<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Eastern Orthodox Church</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Second-largest Christian church</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Eastern Orthodox Church as an institution. For its religion, doctrine and tradition, see <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodoxy</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses of "Orthodox Church", see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church_(disambiguation)" title="Orthodox Church (disambiguation)">Orthodox Church (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodox_Churches" title="Oriental Orthodox Churches">Oriental Orthodox Churches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches" title="Eastern Catholic Churches">Eastern Catholic Churches</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

<li><a href="/wiki/Outline_of_Christianity" title="Outline of Christianity">Outline</a></li></ul>
<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg" title="Christian cross"><img alt="Christian cross" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="18" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/20px-P_christianity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/30px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/40px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template talk:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Christianity_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3774" data-file-width="2023" decoding="async" height="410" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg/220px-Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg/330px-Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg/440px-Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Christ_Pantocrator_(Sinai)" title="Christ Pantocrator (Sinai)">Christ Pantocrator</a></i>, sixth century, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Catherine%27s_Monastery" title="Saint Catherine's Monastery">Saint Catherine's Monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai</a>; the oldest known <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icon</a> of Christ, in one of the oldest monasteries in the world</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Eastern Orthodox Church</b>, also called the <b>Orthodox Church</b>, is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christian_denominations_by_number_of_members" title="List of Christian denominations by number of members">second-largest Christian church</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-8">[7]</a></sup> with approximately 220 million baptized members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-about-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlas_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Atlas-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC1_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC1-10">[9]</a></sup> It operates as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communion_(Christian)" title="Communion (Christian)">communion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a> churches, each governed by its bishops via local <a href="/wiki/Holy_Synod" title="Holy Synod">synods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC1_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC1-10">[9]</a></sup> The church has no central doctrinal or governmental authority analogous to the head of the Roman Catholic Church—the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a>—but the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople</a> is recognized by them as <i><a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">primus inter pares</a></i> ("first among equals") and regarded as the spiritual leader of many of the eastern Christian parishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aboutWeb_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-aboutWeb-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> As one of the oldest surviving religious institutions in the world, the Eastern Orthodox Church has played a prominent role in the history and culture of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southeastern_Europe" title="Southeastern Europe">Southeastern Europe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare19938_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare19938-16">[15]</a></sup> The Eastern Orthodox Church officially calls itself the <b>Orthodox Catholic Church</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ellwood_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-ellwood-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsichlis_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-tsichlis-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Losch2002_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Losch2002-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_theology" title="Eastern Orthodox theology">Eastern Orthodox theology</a> is based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_tradition" title="Holy tradition">holy tradition</a>, which incorporates the dogmatic decrees of the <a href="/wiki/First_seven_ecumenical_councils" title="First seven ecumenical councils">seven ecumenical councils</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scriptures" title="Scriptures">Scriptures</a>, and the teaching of the <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a>. The church teaches that it is the <a href="/wiki/Four_Marks_of_the_Church" title="Four Marks of the Church">one, holy, catholic and apostolic</a> <a href="/wiki/One_true_church" title="One true church">church</a> established by <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Jesus Christ</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Great_Commission" title="Great Commission">Great Commission</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCA_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-OCA-21">[20]</a></sup> and that its bishops are the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_succession" title="Apostolic succession">successors</a> of Christ's <a href="/wiki/Apostles_in_the_New_Testament" title="Apostles in the New Testament">apostles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> It maintains that it practices the original Christian faith, as passed down by holy tradition. Its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarchates" title="Patriarchates">patriarchates</a>, reminiscent of the <a href="/wiki/Pentarchy" title="Pentarchy">pentarchy</a>, and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autocephalous" title="Autocephalous">autocephalous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Autonomy_(Eastern_Orthodoxy)" title="Autonomy (Eastern Orthodoxy)">autonomous</a> churches, reflect a variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hierarchical" title="Hierarchical">hierarchical</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church_organization" title="Eastern Orthodox Church organization">organisation</a>. It recognizes seven major <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacraments</a>, of which the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> is the principal one, celebrated <a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">liturgically</a> in <a href="/wiki/Synaxis" title="Synaxis">synaxis</a>. The church teaches that through <a href="/wiki/Consecration#Eucharist" title="Consecration">consecration</a> <a href="/wiki/Epiclesis" title="Epiclesis">invoked</a> by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Priesthood_(Orthodox_Church)" title="Priesthood (Orthodox Church)">priest</a>, the sacrificial bread and wine become the body and blood of Christ. The <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, mother of Jesus">Virgin Mary</a> is <a href="/wiki/Veneration" title="Veneration">venerated</a> in the Eastern Orthodox Church as the <a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">God-bearer</a>, honored in <a href="/wiki/Marian_devotions" title="Marian devotions">devotions</a>.
</p><p>The churches of Constantinople, Alexandria, Jerusalem, and Antioch—except for some breaks of communion such as the <a href="/wiki/Photian_schism" title="Photian schism">Photian schism</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Acacian_schism" title="Acacian schism">Acacian schism</a>—<a href="/wiki/Koinonia#Between_churches" title="Koinonia">shared communion</a> with the Church of Rome until the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a> in 1054. The 1054 schism was the culmination of mounting theological, political, and cultural disputes, particularly over the <a href="/wiki/Papal_supremacy" title="Papal supremacy">authority of the pope</a>, between those churches. Before the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ephesus" title="Council of Ephesus">Council of Ephesus</a> in AD 431, the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> also shared in this communion, as did the various <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodox_Churches" title="Oriental Orthodox Churches">Oriental Orthodox Churches</a> before the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> in AD 451, all separating primarily over <a href="/wiki/Chalcedonian_Definition" title="Chalcedonian Definition">differences in</a> <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a>.
</p><p>The majority of Eastern Orthodox Christians live mainly in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Europe" title="Southeast Europe">Southeast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> region, <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">Russian Far East</a>. Roughly half of Eastern Orthodox Christians live in the <a href="/wiki/Post-Soviet_states" title="Post-Soviet states">post-Soviet states</a>, mostly Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> There are also communities in the former <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine regions</a> of Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a>, and in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, which are decreasing due to forced migration driven by increased <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians" title="Persecution of Christians">religious persecution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K4D_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-K4D-26">[25]</a></sup> Eastern Orthodox communities are also present in many other parts of the world, particularly North America, Western Europe, and Australia, formed through <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">diaspora</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Christianity" title="Conversion to Christianity">conversions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionary</a> activity.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_characteristics">Name and characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name and characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Eastern Orthodox Church is defined as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Christians" title="Eastern Christians">Eastern Christians</a> which recognize the <a href="/wiki/First_seven_ecumenical_councils" title="First seven ecumenical councils">seven ecumenical councils</a> and usually are in <a href="/wiki/Koinonia" title="Koinonia">communion</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarchate</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Greek Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Patriarchate of Alexandria</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church_of_Antioch" title="Greek Orthodox Church of Antioch">Patriarchate of Antioch</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church_of_Jerusalem" title="Greek Orthodox Church of Jerusalem">Patriarchate of Jerusalem</a>. The Eastern Orthodox churches "are defined positively by their adherence to the <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogmatic</a> definitions of the seven [ecumenical] councils, by the strong sense of not being a <a href="/wiki/Sect" title="Sect">sect</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">denomination</a> but simply continuing the <a href="/wiki/One_true_church" title="One true church">Christian church</a>, and, despite their varied origins, by adherence to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_rite" title="Byzantine rite">Byzantine rite</a>." Those churches are negatively defined by their rejection of <a href="/wiki/Papal_supremacy" title="Papal supremacy">papal immediate and universal supremacy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-27">[26]</a></sup> The seven ecumenical councils recognized by the Eastern Orthodox churches are: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicaea_I" title="Nicaea I">Nicaea I</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">Constantinople I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ephesus" title="Council of Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Chalcedon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Second Council of Constantinople">Constantinople II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Third_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Third Council of Constantinople">Constantinople III</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicaea_II" title="Nicaea II">Nicaea II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:112_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-:112-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-29">[28]</a></sup> Those churches consider the <a href="/wiki/Quinisext_Council" title="Quinisext Council">Quinisext Council</a> "shar[es] the ecumenical authority of Constantinople III.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-:12-29">[28]</a></sup> "By an agreement that appears to be in place in the [Eastern] Orthodox world, possibly the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Council_of_Constantinople_(Eastern_Orthodox)" title="Fourth Council of Constantinople (Eastern Orthodox)">council held in 879</a> to vindicate the <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Patriarch Photius</a> will at some future date be recognized as the eighth [ecumenical] council" by the Eastern Orthodox Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:112_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-:112-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Western_Rite_Orthodoxy" title="Western Rite Orthodoxy">Western Rite Orthodoxy</a> exists both outside and inside Eastern Orthodoxy. Within Eastern Orthodoxy, it is practised by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vicariate" title="Vicariate">vicariate</a> of the Antiochian Orthodox church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In keeping with the church's teaching on universality and with the Nicene Creed, Eastern Orthodox authorities such as <a href="/wiki/Raphael_of_Brooklyn" title="Raphael of Brooklyn">Raphael of Brooklyn</a> have insisted that the full name of the church has always included the term "<a href="/wiki/Catholic_(term)" title="Catholic (term)">Catholic</a>", as in "Holy Orthodox Catholic Apostolic Church".<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTECleenewerck2009[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoeKOUb6OcG4CpgPA101Holy_100–101]._The_word_"Catholic"_shows_that_the_church_consider_itself_[[Catholicity|universal_and_complete]],_and_not_to_be_confused_with_Catholicism._"The_Church_of_the_East_has_never_from_the_first_been_known_by_any_other_name_than_Catholic,_nor_has_she_set_aside_this_title_in_any_official_document."_31-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTECleenewerck2009[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoeKOUb6OcG4CpgPA101Holy_100–101]._The_word_"Catholic"_shows_that_the_church_consider_itself_[[Catholicity|universal_and_complete]],_and_not_to_be_confused_with_Catholicism._"The_Church_of_the_East_has_never_from_the_first_been_known_by_any_other_name_than_Catholic,_nor_has_she_set_aside_this_title_in_any_official_document."-31'>[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The official name of the Eastern Orthodox Church is the "Orthodox Catholic Church".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ellwood_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-ellwood-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsichlis_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-tsichlis-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Losch2002_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Losch2002-20">[19]</a></sup> It is the name by which the church refers to itself<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> and which is issued in its liturgical or <a href="/wiki/Canon_law_of_the_Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Canon law of the Eastern Orthodox Church">canonical</a> texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMerriam-Webster_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions1999[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidZP_f9icf2roCpg309vonepage_309]:_&quot;The_official_designation_of_the_church_in_Eastern_Orthodox_liturgical_or_canonical_texts_is_'the_Orthodox_Catholic_Church.&lt;span_style=&quot;padding-right:.15em;&quot;&gt;'&lt;/span&gt;&quot;_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMerriam-Webster_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions1999[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidZP_f9icf2roCpg309vonepage_309]:_&quot;The_official_designation_of_the_church_in_Eastern_Orthodox_liturgical_or_canonical_texts_is_'the_Orthodox_Catholic_Church.&lt;span_style=&quot;padding-right:.15em;&quot;&gt;'&lt;/span&gt;&quot;-41">[40]</a></sup> Eastern Orthodox theologians refer to the church as catholic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993307_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993307-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzgerald1998[httpsbooksgooglecomid_F5yntZocGICpgPA8_8]_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzgerald1998[httpsbooksgooglecomid_F5yntZocGICpgPA8_8]-43">[42]</a></sup> This name and longer variants containing "Catholic" are also recognized and referenced in other books and publications by secular or non-Eastern Orthodox writers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Vie1945_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Vie1945-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENielsenJohnsonEllis2001248_"In_the_Eastern_Orthodox_Catholic_Church,_authority"_45-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENielsenJohnsonEllis2001248_"In_the_Eastern_Orthodox_Catholic_Church,_authority"-45'>[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortescue1908[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_UPr1ZCxPW6QCpagen290_255]_"it_is_all_gathered_together_and_still_lives_in_the_Holy_Apostolic_Orthodox_Catholic_Church_of_the_Seven_Councils."_46-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortescue1908[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_UPr1ZCxPW6QCpagen290_255]_"it_is_all_gathered_together_and_still_lives_in_the_Holy_Apostolic_Orthodox_Catholic_Church_of_the_Seven_Councils."-46'>[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchadé_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions2006_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchadé_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions2006-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELosch2002[httpsbooksgooglecomidOdKCVjCschMCpgPA76_76]_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELosch2002[httpsbooksgooglecomidOdKCVjCschMCpgPA76_76]-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMerriam-Webster_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions1999[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidZP_f9icf2roCpg309_309–310]_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMerriam-Webster_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions1999[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidZP_f9icf2roCpg309_309–310]-49">[48]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Catechism" title="Catechism">catechism</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philaret_(Drozdov)_of_Moscow" title="Philaret (Drozdov) of Moscow">Philaret (Drozdov) of Moscow</a> published in the 19th century is titled: <i>The Longer Catechism of the Orthodox, Catholic, Eastern Church</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru">Пространный христианский катехизис православныя, кафолическия восточныя Церкви</span>).
</p><p>The common name of the church, "Eastern Orthodox Church", is a shortened practicality that helps to avoid confusions in casual use.
</p><p>From ancient times through the first millennium, Greek was the most prevalent shared language in the demographic regions where the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> flourished, and Greek, being the language in which the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> was written, was the primary liturgical language of the church. For this reason, the eastern churches were sometimes identified as "Greek" (in contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">"Roman" or "Latin" church</a>, which used a Latin translation of the Bible), even before the Great Schism of 1054. After 1054, "Greek Orthodox" or "Greek Catholic" marked a church as being in communion with Constantinople, much as "Catholic" did for communion with the Catholic Church. In Hungarian, the church is still commonly called "Eastern Greek" (<a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a>: <i lang="hu">Görögkeleti</i>). This identification with Greek, however, became increasingly confusing with time. Missionaries brought Eastern Orthodoxy to many regions without ethnic Greeks, where the Greek language was not spoken. In addition, struggles between Rome and Constantinople to control parts of <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Europe" title="Southeast Europe">Southeastern Europe</a> resulted in the conversion of some churches to the Catholic Church, which then also used "Greek Catholic" to indicate their continued use of the Byzantine rites. Today, many of those same churches remain, while a very large number of Eastern Orthodox are not of Greek national origin, and do not use Greek as the language of worship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEncyclopædia_Britannica_Online[httpswwwbritannicacomEBcheckedtopic177174Eastern-Orthodoxy_&quot;Eastern_Orthodoxy&quot;]_&quot;Because_of_the_use_of_the_name_'Greek_Catholics'_by_the_Eastern_churches_of_the_Catholic_Church_and_the_historical_links_of_the_Orthodox_Catholic_church_with_the_Eastern_Roman_Empire_and_Byzantium_(Constantinople),_however,_the_[[exonym]]s_in_American_English_usage_referred_to_it_as_the_'Eastern'_or_'Greek_Orthodox'_Church._These_terms_are_sometimes_misleading,_especially_when_applied_to_Russian_or_Slavic_churches_and_to_the_Orthodox_communities_in_western_Europe_and_America.&quot;_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEncyclopædia_Britannica_Online[httpswwwbritannicacomEBcheckedtopic177174Eastern-Orthodoxy_&quot;Eastern_Orthodoxy&quot;]_&quot;Because_of_the_use_of_the_name_'Greek_Catholics'_by_the_Eastern_churches_of_the_Catholic_Church_and_the_historical_links_of_the_Orthodox_Catholic_church_with_the_Eastern_Roman_Empire_and_Byzantium_(Constantinople),_however,_the_[[exonym]]s_in_American_English_usage_referred_to_it_as_the_'Eastern'_or_'Greek_Orthodox'_Church._These_terms_are_sometimes_misleading,_especially_when_applied_to_Russian_or_Slavic_churches_and_to_the_Orthodox_communities_in_western_Europe_and_America.&quot;-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>"Eastern", then, indicates the geographical element in the church's origin and development, while "Orthodox" indicates the faith, as well as communion with the Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calivas1983_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calivas1983-52">[51]</a></sup> There are additional Christian churches in the east that are in communion with neither the Catholic Church nor the Eastern Orthodox Church, who tend to be distinguished by the category named "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodox" title="Oriental Orthodox">Oriental Orthodox</a>". While the Eastern Orthodox Church continues officially to call itself "Catholic", for reasons of <a href="/wiki/Catholicity" title="Catholicity">universality</a>, the common title of "Eastern Orthodox Church" avoids casual confusion with the Roman Catholic Church.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthodoxy">Orthodoxy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Orthodoxy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2027" data-file-width="1576" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg/220px-Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg/330px-Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg/440px-Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Emperor Constantine</a> presents a representation of the city of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> as tribute to an enthroned Mary and baby Jesus in this church mosaic (<a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, c. 1000)</div></div></div>
<p>The first known use of the phrase "the catholic Church" (<i>he katholike ekklesia</i>) occurred in a letter written about 110 AD from one Greek church to another (<a href="/wiki/Ignatius_of_Antioch" title="Ignatius of Antioch">Ignatius of Antioch</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrnaeans</a>). The letter states: "Wheresoever the bishop shall appear, there let the people be, even as where Jesus may be, there is the universal [katholike] Church."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CathEnc_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-CathEnc-53">[52]</a></sup> Thus, almost from the beginning, Christians referred to the Christian Church as the "one, holy, catholic (from the Greek καθολική, 'according to the whole, universal'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardon1981[httpsbooksgooglecomid3Ke37zpSv3gCpgPA217_217]_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardon1981[httpsbooksgooglecomid3Ke37zpSv3gCpgPA217_217]-54">[53]</a></sup>) and apostolic Church".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCA_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-OCA-21">[20]</a></sup> The Eastern Orthodox Church claims that it is today the continuation and preservation of that same early church.
</p><p>A number of other Christian churches also make a similar claim: the Roman <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Oriental Orthodox</a>. In the Eastern Orthodox view, the Assyrians and Orientals left the Orthodox Church in the years following the Third Ecumenical <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ephesus" title="Council of Ephesus">Council of Ephesus</a> (431) and the Fourth Ecumenical <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> (451), respectively, in their refusal to accept those councils' <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christological</a> definitions. Similarly, the churches in Rome and Constantinople separated in an event known as the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a>, traditionally dated to the year 1054, although it was more a gradual process than a sudden break.
</p><p>To all these churches, the claim to <a href="/wiki/Catholicity" title="Catholicity">catholicity</a> (universality, oneness with the ancient Church) is important for multiple doctrinal reasons that have more bearing internally in each church than in their relation to the others, now separated in faith. The meaning of holding to a faith that is true is the primary reason why anyone's statement of which church split off from which other has any significance at all; the issues go as deep as the schisms. The depth of this meaning in the Eastern Orthodox Church is registered first in its use of the word "<a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">Orthodox</a>" itself, a union of <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>orthos</i> ("straight", "correct", "true", "right") and <i><a href="/wiki/Doxa" title="Doxa">doxa</a></i> ("common belief", from the ancient verb δοκέω-δοκῶ which is translated "to believe", "to think", "to consider", "to imagine", "to assume").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The dual meanings of <i>doxa</i>, with "glory" or "glorification" (of God by the church and of the church by God), especially in worship, yield the pair "correct belief" and "true worship". Together, these express the core of a fundamental teaching about the inseparability of belief and worship and their role in drawing the church together with Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare199116,_271_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare199116,_271-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHierotheos199869–72_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHierotheos199869–72-57">[56]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgarian</a> and all the <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic</a> churches use the title <i>Pravoslavie</i> (<a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_alphabets" title="Cyrillic alphabets">Cyrillic</a>: Православие), meaning "correctness of glorification", to denote what is in English <i>Orthodoxy</i>, while the Georgians use the title <i>Martlmadidebeli</i>.
</p><p>The term "Eastern Church" (the geographic east in the East–West Schism) has been used to distinguish it from <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">western Christendom</a> (the geographic West, which at first came to designate the Catholic communion, later also the various <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> and Anglican branches). "Eastern" is used to indicate that the highest concentrations of the Eastern Orthodox Church presence remain in the eastern part of the Christian world, although it is growing worldwide. Orthodox Christians throughout the world use various ethnic or national jurisdictional titles, or more inclusively, the title "Eastern Orthodox", "Orthodox Catholic", or simply "Orthodox".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calivas1983_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Calivas1983-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>What unites Orthodox Christians is the catholic faith as carried through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_tradition" title="Holy tradition">holy tradition</a>. That faith is expressed most fundamentally in scripture and worship,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991212–213_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991212–213-58">[57]</a></sup> and the latter most essentially through baptism and in the <a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">Divine Liturgy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991282_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991282-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>The lines of even this test can blur, however, when differences that arise are not due to doctrine, but to recognition of jurisdiction. As the Eastern Orthodox Church has spread into the west and over the world, the church as a whole has yet to sort out all the inter-jurisdictional issues that have arisen in the expansion, leaving some areas of doubt about what is proper church governance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991180–199_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991180–199-60">[59]</a></sup> Moreover, as in the ancient church persecutions, the aftermath of <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Persecution of Christians in the Soviet Union">persecutions of Christians in communist nations</a> has left behind some issues of governance and lapse piety that have yet to be completely resolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991152–179_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991152–179-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>All members of the Eastern Orthodox Church profess the same faith, regardless of race or nationality, jurisdiction or local custom, or century of birth. Holy tradition encompasses the understandings and means by which that unity of faith is transmitted across boundaries of time, geography, and culture. It is a continuity that exists only inasmuch as it lives within Christians themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991203–204_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991203–204-62">[61]</a></sup> It is not static, nor an observation of rules, but rather a sharing of observations that spring both from within and also in keeping with others, even others who lived lives long past. The church proclaims the Holy Spirit maintains the unity and consistency of holy tradition to preserve the integrity of the faith within the church, as given in the scriptural promises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The shared beliefs of Orthodoxy, and its theology, exist within holy tradition and cannot be separated from it, for their meaning is not expressed in mere words alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991215_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991215-64">[63]</a></sup> Doctrine cannot be understood unless it is prayed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> Doctrine must also be lived in order to be prayed, for without action, the prayer is idle and empty, a mere vanity, and therefore the theology of demons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholicity">Catholicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Catholicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Catholicity_of_the_Orthodox_Church"></span>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3825" data-file-width="2554" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg/200px-John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg/300px-John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg/400px-John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icon</a> of Saint <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>, 14th century, North Macedonia</div></div></div>
<p>The Eastern Orthodox Church considers itself to be both orthodox and catholic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> The doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Catholicity" title="Catholicity">Catholicity of the Church</a>, as derived from the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>, is essential to Eastern Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiology" title="Ecclesiology">ecclesiology</a>. The term <i>Catholicity of the Church</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Καθολικότης τῆς Ἐκκλησίας</span></span>) is used in its original sense, as a designation for the universality of the Christian Church, centered around Christ. Therefore, the Eastern Orthodox notion of catholicity is not centered around any singular see, unlike the Catholic Church which has one earthly center.
</p><p>Due to the influence of the Catholic Church in the west, where the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> itself developed, the words "catholic" and "catholicity" are sometimes used to refer to that church specifically. However, the more prominent dictionary sense given for general use is still the one shared by other languages, implying breadth and universality, reflecting comprehensive scope.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmerican_Heritage_Dict5th_ed294_[httpahdictionarycomwordsearchhtmlqcatholicity_"catholicity"]_68-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmerican_Heritage_Dict5th_ed294_[httpahdictionarycomwordsearchhtmlqcatholicity_"catholicity"]-68'>[67]</a></sup> In a Christian context, the Christian Church, as identified with the original church founded by Christ and his apostles, is said to be catholic (or universal) in regard to its union with Christ in faith. Just as Christ is indivisible, so are union with him and faith in him, whereby the Christian Church is "universal", unseparated, and comprehensive, including all who share that faith. Orthodox bishop <a href="/wiki/Kallistos_Ware" title="Kallistos Ware">Kallistos Ware</a> has called that "simple Christianity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare199116_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare199116-69">[68]</a></sup> That is the sense of early and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patristic" title="Patristic">patristic</a> usage wherein the church usually refers to itself as the "Catholic Church",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEncyclopedia_of_Christianity2003[httpsbooksgooglecomid7ly4DgtT3LkCpgPA867_867]_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEncyclopedia_of_Christianity2003[httpsbooksgooglecomid7ly4DgtT3LkCpgPA867_867]-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeith1982[httpsbooksgooglecomidsaFgJRjaPwcCpgPA486_486]_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeith1982[httpsbooksgooglecomidsaFgJRjaPwcCpgPA486_486]-71">[70]</a></sup> whose faith is the "Orthodox faith". It is also the sense within the phrase "one, holy, catholic, and apostolic Church", found in the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>, and referred to in Orthodox worship, e.g. in the litany of the catechumens in the Divine Liturgy.
</p><p>With the mutual excommunications of the East–West Schism in 1054,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> the churches in Rome and Constantinople each viewed the other as having departed from the <a href="/wiki/One_true_church" title="One true church">true church</a>, leaving a smaller but still-catholic church in place. Each retained the "Catholic" part of its title, the "<i>Roman</i> Catholic Church" (or Catholic Church) on the one hand, and the "<i>Orthodox</i> Catholic Church" on the other, each of which was defined in terms of inter-communion with either Rome or Constantinople. While the Eastern Orthodox Church recognizes what it shares in common with other churches, including the Catholic Church, it sees catholicity in terms of complete union in communion and faith, with the Church throughout all time, and the sharing remains incomplete when not shared fully.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="History of the Eastern Orthodox Church">History of the Eastern Orthodox Church</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Church">Early Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early Church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="1276" decoding="async" height="84" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg/210px-Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg/315px-Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg/420px-Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early Christian "<a href="/wiki/Ichthys" title="Ichthys">Ichthys</a>" (<i>fish</i>) inscription from ancient <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelve_Apostles" title="Twelve Apostles">Apostles</a> traveled extensively throughout the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, including Asia Minor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity" title="Early centers of Christianity">establishing churches in major communities</a>, with the first churches appearing in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_in_Christianity" title="Jerusalem in Christianity">Jerusalem</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a>, then in <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thessalonica" title="Thessalonica">Thessalonica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illyricum_(Roman_province)" title="Illyricum (Roman province)">Illyricum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Byzantium</a>, which centuries later would become prominent as the <a href="/wiki/New_Rome" title="New Rome">New Rome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ware1993_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-ware1993-73">[72]</a></sup> Christianity <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Persecution of Christians in the Roman Empire">encountered considerable resistance in the Roman Empire</a>, mostly because its adherents refused to comply with the demands of the Roman state—often even when their lives were threatened—by offering sacrifices to the pagan gods. Despite persecution, skepticism, and initial social stigma, the Christian Church spread, particularly following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I_and_Christianity" title="Constantine I and Christianity">conversion of Emperor Constantine I</a> in 312 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ware1993_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-ware1993-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_4th_century" title="Christianity in the 4th century">fourth century</a>, Christianity was present in numerous regions well beyond the Levant. A number of influential schools of thought had arisen, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Catechetical_School_of_Alexandria" title="Catechetical School of Alexandria">Alexandrian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/School_of_Antioch" title="School of Antioch">Antiochian</a> philosophical approaches. Other groups, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arians" title="Arians">Arians</a>, had also managed to gain influence. However, their positions caused theological conflicts within the church, thus prompting the Emperor Constantine to call for a great ecumenical synod in order to define the church's position against the growing, often widely diverging, philosophical and theological interpretations of Christianity. He made it possible for this council to meet not only by providing a location, but by offering to pay for the transportation of all the existing bishops of the church. Most modern Christian churches regard this synod, commonly called the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a> or more generally the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Ecumenical_Council" title="First Ecumenical Council">First Ecumenical Council</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ware1993_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-ware1993-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> as of major importance.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecumenical_councils">Ecumenical councils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ecumenical councils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_seven_Ecumenical_Councils" title="First seven Ecumenical Councils">First seven Ecumenical Councils</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">State church of the Roman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nicaea_icon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1388" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nicaea_icon.jpg/220px-Nicaea_icon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nicaea_icon.jpg/330px-Nicaea_icon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nicaea_icon.jpg/440px-Nicaea_icon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nicaea_icon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Icon depicting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">the Emperor Constantine</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishops</a> of the First Council of Nicaea (325) holding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niceno-Constantinopolitan_Creed" title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">Niceno–Constantinopolitan Creed of 381</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Several doctrinal disputes from the fourth century onwards led to the calling of <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_council" title="Ecumenical council">ecumenical councils</a>. In the Orthodox Church, an ecumenical council is the supreme authority that can be invoked to resolve contested issues of the faith. As such, these councils have been held to resolve the most important theological matters that came to be disputed within the Christian Church. Many lesser disagreements were resolved through local councils in the areas where they arose, before they grew significant enough to require an ecumenical council.
</p><p>There are seven councils authoritatively recognised as ecumenical by the Eastern Orthodox Church:
</p>
<ol><li>The <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Ecumenical Council</a> was convoked by the Roman Emperor Constantine at <a href="/wiki/Nicaea" title="Nicaea">Nicaea</a> in 325 and presided over by the Patriarch <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_I_of_Alexandria" title="Pope Alexander I of Alexandria">Alexander of Alexandria</a>, with over 300 bishops condemning the view of <a href="/wiki/Arius" title="Arius">Arius</a> that the Son is a created being inferior to the Father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">Second Ecumenical Council</a> was held at Constantinople in 381, presided over by the Patriarchs of Alexandria and Antioch, with 150 bishops, defining the nature of the Holy Spirit against those asserting His inequality with the other persons of the Trinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Ephesus" title="First Council of Ephesus">Third Ecumenical Council</a> is that of Ephesus in 431, presided over by the Patriarch of Alexandria, with 250 bishops, which affirmed that Mary is truly "Birthgiver" or "Mother" of God (<i><a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a></i>), contrary to the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Nestorius" title="Nestorius">Nestorius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Fourth Ecumenical Council</a> is that of Chalcedon in 451, Patriarch of Constantinople presiding, 500 bishops, affirmed that Jesus is truly God and truly man, without mixture of the two natures, contrary to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophysite" title="Monophysite">Monophysite</a> teaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Second Council of Constantinople">Fifth Ecumenical Council</a> is the second of Constantinople in 553, interpreting the decrees of Chalcedon and further explaining the relationship of the two natures of Jesus; it also condemned the alleged teachings of <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a> on the pre-existence of the soul, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Third_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Third Council of Constantinople">Sixth Ecumenical Council</a> is the third of Constantinople in 681; it declared that Christ has two wills of his two natures, human and divine, contrary to the teachings of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monothelites" title="Monothelites">Monothelites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">Seventh Ecumenical Council</a> was called under the Empress Regent <a href="/wiki/Irene_of_Athens" title="Irene of Athens">Irene of Athens</a> in 787, known as the second of Nicaea. It supports the <a href="/wiki/Veneration" title="Veneration">veneration</a> of <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icons</a> while forbidding their worship. It is often referred to as "The Triumph of Orthodoxy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>There are also two other councils which are considered ecumenical by some Eastern Orthodox:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Council_of_Constantinople_(Eastern_Orthodox)" title="Fourth Council of Constantinople (Eastern Orthodox)">Fourth Council of Constantinople</a> was called in 879. It restored <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Photius</a> to his <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">See in Constantinople</a> and condemned any alteration of the Nicene-Constantinopolitan Creed of 381.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Fifth Council of Constantinople">Fifth Council of Constantinople</a> was actually a series of councils held between 1341 and 1351. It affirmed the <a href="/wiki/Hesychasm" title="Hesychasm">hesychastic</a> theology of <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Palamas" title="Gregory Palamas">Gregory Palamas</a> and condemned the philosopher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barlaam_of_Calabria" title="Barlaam of Calabria">Barlaam of Calabria</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_major_councils">Other major councils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Other major councils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition to these councils, there have been a number of other significant councils meant to further define the Eastern Orthodox position. They are the Synods of Constantinople, in <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Constantinople_(1484)" title="Synod of Constantinople (1484)">1484</a>, 1583, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oros_of_the_Holy_Great_Church_of_Christ" title="Oros of the Holy Great Church of Christ">1755</a>, 1819, and <a href="/wiki/Phyletism" title="Phyletism">1872</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synod_of_Ia%C8%99i" title="Synod of Iași">Synod of Iași</a> in 1642, and the Pan-Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Jerusalem_(1672)" title="Synod of Jerusalem (1672)">Synod of Jerusalem in 1672</a>. Another council convened in <a href="/wiki/Pan-Orthodox_Council" title="Pan-Orthodox Council">June 2016</a> to discuss many modern phenomena, other Christian confessions, Eastern Orthodoxy's relation with other religions and fasting disciplines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Roman.2FByzantine_Empire"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman/Byzantine_Empire">Roman/Byzantine Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Roman/Byzantine Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">State church of the Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3681" data-file-width="5514" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/220px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/330px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/440px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, the largest church in the world and patriarchal basilica of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> for nearly a thousand years, later converted into a <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosque</a>, then a museum, then back to a mosque.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> is generally considered to be the center and the "cradle of Orthodox <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_civilization" title="Christian civilization">Christian civilization</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> From the mid-5th century to the early 13th century, Constantinople was the largest and wealthiest city in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Eastern <a href="/wiki/Christian_culture" title="Christian culture">Christian culture</a> reached its golden age during the high point of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> and continued to flourish in Ukraine and Russia, after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">fall of Constantinople</a>. Numerous <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a> churches were established in Europe: Greece, Georgia, Ukraine, as well as in Russia and Asia.
</p><p>In the 530s the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Church of the Holy Wisdom</a> (Hagia Sophia) was built in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> under Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> Beginning with subsequent <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine architecture</a>, Hagia Sophia became the paradigmatic <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_church_architecture" title="Eastern Orthodox church architecture">Orthodox church form</a> and its architectural style was emulated by <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_architecture#Classical_period_(1437–1703)" title="Ottoman architecture">Ottoman mosques</a> a thousand years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heinle1996_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heinle1996-87">[86]</a></sup> Being the <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_see" title="Episcopal see">episcopal see</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">ecumenical patriarch of Constantinople</a>, it remained the world's largest cathedral for nearly a thousand years, until <a href="/wiki/Seville_Cathedral" title="Seville Cathedral">Seville Cathedral</a> was completed in 1520. <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> has been described as "holding a unique position in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_world" title="Christian world">Christian world</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heinle1996_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heinle1996-87">[86]</a></sup> and architectural and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_icon" title="Cultural icon">cultural icon</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_culture" title="Byzantine culture">Byzantine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Culture" title="Eastern Orthodox Culture">Eastern Orthodox civilization</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M19_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-M19-89">[88]</a></sup> and it is considered the epitome of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine architecture</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAT_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-BAT-90">[89]</a></sup> and is said to have "changed the history of architecture".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes22_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes22-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_schisms">Early schisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Early schisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are the "<a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorian</a>" churches resulted from the reaction of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ephesus" title="Council of Ephesus">Council of Ephesus</a> (431), which are the earliest surviving Eastern Christian churches that keep the faith of only the first two ecumenical councils, i.e., the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a> (325) and the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">First Council of Constantinople</a> (381) as legitimate. "Nestorian" is an outsider's term for a tradition that predated the influence of <a href="/wiki/Nestorius" title="Nestorius">Nestorius</a>, the origin of which might lay in certain sections of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/School_of_Antioch" title="School of Antioch">School of Antioch</a> or via Nestorius' teachers <a href="/wiki/Theodore_of_Mopsuestia" title="Theodore of Mopsuestia">Theodore of Mopsuestia</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diodore_of_Tarsus" title="Diodore of Tarsus">Diodore of Tarsus</a>. The modern incarnation of the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Church" title="Nestorian Church">Nestorian Church</a>" is commonly referred to as "the Assyrian Church" or fully as the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a>.
</p><p>The church in Egypt (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarchate_of_Alexandria" title="Patriarchate of Alexandria">Patriarchate of Alexandria</a>) split into two groups following the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> (451), over a dispute about the relation between the divine and human natures of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>. Eventually this led to each group <a href="/wiki/Anathema#Religious_usage" title="Anathema">anathematizing</a> the other. Those that remained in communion with the other patriarchs (by accepting the Council of Chalcedon) are known today as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Greek Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Greek Orthodox Church of Alexandria</a>, where the adjective "Greek" refers to their ties to the Greek-speaking culture of the Byzantine Empire. However, those who disagreed with the findings of the Council of Chalcedon were the majority in Egypt, and today they are known as the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church" title="Coptic Orthodox Church">Coptic Orthodox Church</a>, having maintained a separate patriarchate. The Coptic Orthodox Church is currently the largest Christian church in Egypt and in the whole Middle East. There was also a similar, albeit smaller scale, split in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarchate_of_Antioch" title="Patriarchate of Antioch">Patriarchate of Antioch</a>), which resulted in the separation of the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church_of_Antioch" title="Greek Orthodox Church of Antioch">Byzantine Patriarchate of Antioch</a>.
</p><p>Those who disagreed with the Council of Chalcedon are sometimes called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Oriental Orthodox</a>" to distinguish them from the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a>", who accepted the Council of Chalcedon. Oriental Orthodox are also sometimes referred to as "non-Chalcedonians", or "anti-Chalcedonians". The Oriental Orthodox Church denies that it is monophysite and prefers the term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miaphysite" title="Miaphysite">miaphysite</a>", to denote the "united" nature of Jesus (two natures united into one) consistent with Cyril's theology: "The term union ... signifies the concurrence in one reality of those things which are understood to be united" and "the Word who is ineffably united with it in a manner beyond all description" (<a href="/wiki/Cyril_of_Alexandria" title="Cyril of Alexandria">Cyril of Alexandria</a>, <i>On the Unity of Christ</i>). This is also defined in the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church" title="Coptic Orthodox Church">Coptic</a> liturgy, where it is mentioned "He made it [his humanity] one with his divinity without mingling, without confusion and without alteration", and "His divinity parted not from his humanity for a single moment nor a twinkling of an eye."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> They do not accept the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Eutyches" title="Eutyches">Eutyches</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Eutychianism" title="Eutychianism">Eutychianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> Both the Eastern Orthodox and Oriental Orthodox churches formally believe themselves to be the continuation of the true church.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversion_of_South_and_East_Slavs">Conversion of South and East Slavs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Conversion of South and East Slavs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Bulgaria" title="Christianization of Bulgaria">Christianization of Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_the_Rus%27_Khaganate" title="Christianization of the Rus' Khaganate">Christianization of the Rus' Khaganate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Kievan_Rus%27" title="Christianization of Kievan Rus'">Christianization of Kievan Rus'</a></div>
<p>In the ninth and tenth centuries, Christianity made great inroads into pagan Europe, including <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Empire" title="Bulgarian Empire">Bulgaria</a> (864) and later <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a> (988). This work was made possible by  <a href="/wiki/Cyril_and_Methodius" title="Cyril and Methodius">Cyril and Methodius</a> of <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a>, two brothers chosen by <a href="/wiki/Michael_III" title="Michael III">Byzantine emperor Michael III</a> to fulfill the request of <a href="/wiki/Rastislav_of_Moravia" title="Rastislav of Moravia">Rastislav of Moravia</a> for teachers who could minister to the Moravians in their own language. Cyril and Methodius began translating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine_liturgy" title="Divine liturgy">divine liturgy</a>, other liturgical texts, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gospels" title="Gospels">Gospels</a> along with some other <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">scriptural texts</a> into local languages; with time, as these translations were copied by speakers of other dialects, the hybrid literary language <a href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic" title="Church Slavonic">Church Slavonic</a> was created. Originally sent to convert the Slavs of <a href="/wiki/Great_Moravia" title="Great Moravia">Great Moravia</a>, Cyril and Methodius were forced to compete with <a href="/wiki/Frankish_mythology" title="Frankish mythology">Frankish</a> missionaries from the Roman diocese; their <a href="/wiki/Saint_Sava_(disciple_of_Saints_Cyril_and_Methodius)" title="Saint Sava (disciple of Saints Cyril and Methodius)">disciples</a> were driven out of Great Moravia in AD 886 and emigrated to <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Empire" title="Bulgarian Empire">Bulgaria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="309" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg/280px-Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg/420px-Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg/560px-Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The baptism of <a href="/wiki/Olga_of_Kiev" title="Olga of Kiev">Princess Olga</a> in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a></i>, a miniature from the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radzivill_Chronicle" title="Radzivill Chronicle">Radzivill Chronicle</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Bulgaria" title="Christianization of Bulgaria">Christianisation of Bulgaria</a> in 864, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Angelar" title="Saint Angelar">the disciples</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cyril_and_Methodius" title="Cyril and Methodius">Cyril and Methodius</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Empire" title="Bulgarian Empire">Bulgaria</a>, the most important being <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Ohrid" title="Clement of Ohrid">Clement of Ohrid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Naum" title="Saint Naum">Naum of Preslav</a>, were of great importance to the Orthodox faith in the <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">First Bulgarian Empire</a>. In a short time they managed to prepare and instruct the future <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">Bulgarian</a> clergy into the biblical texts and in 870 AD the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourth_Council_of_Constantinople_(Roman_Catholic)" title="Fourth Council of Constantinople (Roman Catholic)">Fourth Council of Constantinople</a> granted the Bulgarians the oldest organised <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autocephalous" title="Autocephalous">autocephalous</a> Slavic Orthodox Church, which shortly thereafter became Patriarchate. The success of the <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Bulgaria" title="Christianization of Bulgaria">conversion of the Bulgarians</a> facilitated the conversion of East <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic peoples</a>, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Rus%27_people" title="Rus' people">Rus'</a>, predecessors of <a href="/wiki/Belarusians" title="Belarusians">Belarusians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians" title="Ukrainians">Ukrainians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> A major event in this effort was the development of the <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic script</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Preslav_Literary_School" title="Preslav Literary School">Preslav Literary School</a> in the ninth century; this script, along with the liturgical <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavonic</a>, also called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Bulgarian" title="Old Bulgarian">Old Bulgarian</a>, were declared official in <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> in 893.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>The work of Cyril and Methodius and their disciples had a major impact on the <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a> as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cyril_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cyril-101">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> They accepted Christianity collectively along familial and tribal lines, a gradual process that occurred between the seventh and ninth centuries. In commemoration of their baptisms, each Serbian family or tribe began to celebrate an exclusively Serbian custom called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slava_(patron_saint)" title="Slava (patron saint)">Slava (patron saint)</a> in a special way to honor the saint on whose day they received the sacrament of <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a>. It is the most solemn day of the year for all Serbs of the Orthodox faith and has played a role of vital importance in the history of the Serbian people. Slava remains a celebration of the conversion of the Serbian people, which the church blessed and proclaimed a church institution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup>
