</p><p>The missionaries to the East and <a href="/wiki/South_Slavs" title="South Slavs">South Slavs</a> had great success in part because they used the people's native language rather than <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, the predominant language of the Byzantine Empire, or <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, as the Roman priests did.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cyril_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cyril-101">[100]</a></sup> Perhaps the greatest legacy of their efforts is the Russian Orthodox Church, which is the largest of the Orthodox churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Great_Schism_.281054.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Schism_(1054)">Great Schism (1054)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Great Schism (1054)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a></div>
<p>In the 11th century, what was recognised as the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">Great Schism</a> took place between <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, which led to separation between the Church of the West, the Catholic Church, and the Eastern Byzantine churches, now the Orthodox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> There were doctrinal issues like the <a href="/wiki/Filioque" title="Filioque">filioque</a> clause and the authority of the Roman <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a> involved in the split, but these were greatly exacerbated by political factors of both Church and state, and by cultural and linguistic differences between Latins and Greeks. Regarding <a href="/wiki/Papal_supremacy" title="Papal supremacy">papal supremacy</a>, the Eastern half grew disillusioned with the Pope's centralisation of power, as well as his blatant attempts of excluding the Eastern half in regard to papal approvals. It used to be that the emperor would at least have say when a new Pope would be elected, but towards the high Middle Ages, the Christians in Rome were slowly consolidating power and removing Byzantine influence. However, even before this exclusionary tendency from the West, well before 1054, the Eastern and Western halves of the Church were in perpetual conflict, particularly during the periods of Eastern <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">iconoclasm</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Photian_schism" title="Photian schism">Photian schism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1315" data-file-width="1367" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg/220px-Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg/330px-Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg/440px-Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Latin Crusaders <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(1204)" title="Siege of Constantinople (1204)">sacking the city of Constantinople</a>, the capital of the Eastern Orthodox controlled <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, in 1204.</div></div></div>
<p>The final breach is often considered to have arisen after the capture and sacking of Constantinople by the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a> in 1204; the final break with Rome occurred circa 1450. The sacking of <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Church of Holy Wisdom</a> and establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a> as a seeming attempt to supplant the Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> in 1204 is viewed with some rancour to the present day. In 2004, <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a> extended a formal apology for the sacking of Constantinople in 1204, which had also been strongly condemned by the Pope at the time, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_III" title="Pope Innocent III">Innocent III</a>; the apology was formally accepted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Bartholomew_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Bartholomew of Constantinople">Patriarch Bartholomew of Constantinople</a>. However, many items stolen during this time, such as <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">holy relics</a> and riches, are still held in various European cities, particularly <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reunion was attempted twice, at the 1274 <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Lyon" title="Second Council of Lyon">Second Council of Lyon</a> and the 1439 <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Florence" title="Council of Florence">Council of Florence</a>. The Council of Florence briefly reestablished communion between East and West, which lasted until after the fall of Constantinople in 1453. In each case, however, the councils were rejected by the Orthodox people as a whole, and the union of Florence also became very politically difficult after Constantinople came under Ottoman rule. However, in the time since, several local Orthodox Christian churches have renewed union with Rome, known as the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches" title="Eastern Catholic Churches">Eastern Catholic Churches</a>. Recent decades have seen a renewal of ecumenical spirit and dialogue between the Catholic and Orthodox churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_Church_under_Ottoman_rule">Greek Church under Ottoman rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Greek Church under Ottoman rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Christianity in the Ottoman Empire">Christianity in the Ottoman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Eastern_Orthodox_Church_under_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="History of the Eastern Orthodox Church under the Ottoman Empire">History of the Eastern Orthodox Church under the Ottoman Empire</a></div>
<p>The Byzantine Empire never fully recovered from the sack of Constantinople in 1204. Over the next two centuries, it entered a precipitous decline in both territory and influence. In 1453, a much-diminished Byzantine Empire fell to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, ending what was once the most powerful state in the Orthodox Christian world, if not in all <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>. By this time <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, another major center of Eastern Christianity, had been under <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslim</a> control for some seven centuries; most Eastern Orthodox communities <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_conquest_of_the_Balkans" title="Ottoman conquest of the Balkans">across southeastern Europe</a> gradually came under Ottoman rule by the 16th century.
</p><p>Under the Ottomans, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church_of_Constantinople" title="Orthodox Church of Constantinople">Greek Orthodox Church</a> acquired substantial power as an autonomous <i><a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">millet</a></i>. The ecumenical patriarch was the religious and administrative ruler of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R%C3%BBm" title="Rûm">Rûm</a></i>, an Ottoman administrative unit meaning "Roman", which encompassed all <a href="/wiki/Rum_Millet" title="Rum Millet">Orthodox subjects of the Empire</a> regardless of ethnicity. While legally subordinate to Muslims and <a href="/wiki/Dhimmi" title="Dhimmi">subject to various restrictions</a>, the Orthodox community was generally tolerated and left to govern its own internal affairs, both religiously and legally. Until the empire's dissolution in the early 20th century, Orthodox Christians would remain the largest non-Muslim minority, and at times among the wealthiest and most politically influential.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Smyrna-massacre_greeks-killed_line.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="403" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Smyrna-massacre_greeks-killed_line.jpg/220px-Smyrna-massacre_greeks-killed_line.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Smyrna-massacre_greeks-killed_line.jpg/330px-Smyrna-massacre_greeks-killed_line.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Smyrna-massacre_greeks-killed_line.jpg/440px-Smyrna-massacre_greeks-killed_line.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Smyrna-massacre_greeks-killed_line.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greek Orthodox massacred during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Genocide" title="Greek Genocide">Greek Genocide</a> in Smyrna in 1922.</div></div></div>
<p>During the period 1914-1923 in Asia Minor (Anatolia) the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Genocide" title="Greek Genocide">Greek Genocide</a> took place by the Ottomans. During the Greek Genocide, many Orthodox Christians were persecuted and martyred. The culmination of the martyrdom was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor_Catastrophe" title="Asia Minor Catastrophe">Asia Minor Catastrophe</a> with the killing of a large number of Orthodox. Among them, 347 clergymen of the Smyrna region and <a href="/wiki/Chrysostomos_of_Smyrna" title="Chrysostomos of Smyrna"> Metropolitan of Smyrna Chrysostomos</a> were tortured and martyred. The period 1923-1924 was followed by the obligatory <a href="/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Greece_and_Turkey" title="Population exchange between Greece and Turkey">Population exchange between Greece and Turkey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russian_Orthodox_Church_in_the_Russian_Empire">Russian Orthodox Church in the Russian Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Russian Orthodox Church in the Russian Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the time most Orthodox communities came under Muslim rule in the mid 15th century, Orthodoxy was very strong in Russia, which had maintained close cultural and political ties with the Byzantine Empire; roughly two decades after the fall of Constantinople, <a href="/wiki/Ivan_III_of_Russia" title="Ivan III of Russia">Ivan III of Russia</a> married <a href="/wiki/Sophia_Palaiologina" title="Sophia Palaiologina">Sophia Palaiologina</a>, a niece of the last Byzantine Emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_XI_Palaiologos" title="Constantine XI Palaiologos">Constantine XI</a>, and styled himself Tsar ("Caesar") or <i>imperator</i>. In 1547, his grandson <a href="/wiki/Ivan_the_Terrible" title="Ivan the Terrible">Ivan IV</a>, a devout Orthodox Christian, cemented the title as "Tsar of All Rus", establishing Russia's first centralised state with divinely appointed rulers. In 1589, the Patriarchate of Constantinople granted autocephalous status to Moscow, the capital of what was now the largest Orthodox Christian polity; the city thereafter referred to itself as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Rome" title="Third Rome">Third Rome</a><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Rome#Russian_claims" title="Third Rome">—</a>the cultural and religious heir of Constantinople.
</p><p>Until 1666, when Patriarch Nikon was deposed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_tsar" title="Russian tsar">tsar</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> had been independent of the State.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> In 1721, the first Russian Emperor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_I_of_Russia" title="Peter I of Russia">Peter I</a>, abolished completely the patriarchate and effectively made the church a department of the government, ruled by a <a href="/wiki/Most_Holy_Synod" title="Most Holy Synod">most holy synod</a> composed of senior bishops and lay bureaucrats appointed by the Emperor himself. Over time, Imperial Russia would style itself a protector and patron of all Orthodox Christians, especially those within the Ottoman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>For nearly 200 years, until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevik" title="Bolshevik">Bolsheviks</a>' <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">October Revolution</a> of 1917, the Russian Orthodox Church remained, in effect, a governmental agency and an instrument of tsarist rule. It was used to varying degrees in imperial campaigns of <a href="/wiki/Russification" title="Russification">Russification</a>, and was even allowed to levy <a href="/wiki/Tax" title="Tax">taxes</a> on <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasants</a>. The church's close ties with the state came to a head under Nicholas I (1825-1855), who explicitly made Orthodoxy a <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy,_Autocracy,_and_Nationality" title="Orthodoxy, Autocracy, and Nationality">core doctrine of imperial unity and legitimacy</a>. The Orthodox faith became further tied to Russian identity and nationalism, while the church was further subordinated to the interests of the state. Consequently, Russian Orthodox Church, along with the imperial regime to which it belonged, came to be presented as an <a href="/wiki/Enemy_of_the_people" title="Enemy of the people">enemy of the people</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolsheviks</a> and other Russian revolutionaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Orthodox_churches_under_Communist_rule">Eastern Orthodox churches under Communist rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Eastern Orthodox churches under Communist rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Persecution of Christians in the Soviet Union">Persecution of Christians in the Soviet Union</a></div>

demolition of the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour" title="Cathedral of Christ the Saviour">Cathedral of Christ the Saviour</a> in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg"><img alt="Large church" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg/200px-Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg/300px-Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg/400px-Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">The rebuilt Cathedral of Christ the Saviour, currently the second tallest Orthodox church</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/October_revolution" title="October revolution">October revolution</a> of 1917, part of the clergy of the Russian Orthodox Church fled abroad to escape <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevik" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> persecutions, founding an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church_Outside_Russia" title="Russian Orthodox Church Outside Russia">independent church in exile</a>, which reunified with its Russian counterpart in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> Some actions against Orthodox priests and believers along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Execution" title="Execution">execution</a> included <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a>, being sent to <a href="/wiki/Gulag" title="Gulag">prison camps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sharashka" title="Sharashka">labour camps</a> or <a href="/wiki/Psikhushka" title="Psikhushka">mental hospitals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blqarv_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-blqarv-121">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> In the first five years after the Bolshevik revolution, 28 bishops and 1,200 priests were executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-time-123">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>After Nazi Germany's attack on the Soviet Union in 1941, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> revived the Russian Orthodox Church to intensify patriotic support for the war effort. By 1957 about 22,000 Russian Orthodox churches had become active. However, in 1959, <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> initiated his own campaign against the Russian Orthodox Church and forced the closure of about 12,000 churches. It is estimated that 50,000 clergy had been executed between the revolution and the end of the Khrushchev era. Members of the church hierarchy were jailed or forced out, their places taken by docile clergy, many of whom had ties with the KGB. By 1985 fewer than 7,000 churches remained active.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-time-123">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> was the only state to have declared itself <a href="/wiki/State_atheism" title="State atheism">officially fully atheist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> In some other Communist states such as Romania, the <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Orthodox_Church" title="Romanian Orthodox Church">Romanian Orthodox Church</a> as an organisation enjoyed relative freedom and even prospered, albeit under strict secret police control. That, however, did not rule out demolishing churches and monasteries as part of broader <a href="/wiki/Systematization_(Romania)" title="Systematization (Romania)">systematisation</a> (urban planning), and state persecution of individual believers. As an example of the latter, Romania stands out as a country which ran a <a href="/wiki/Pite%C8%99ti_Prison" title="Pitești Prison">specialised institution</a> where many Orthodox (along with people of other faiths) were subjected to <a href="/wiki/Psychological_punishment" title="Psychological punishment">psychological punishment</a> or torture and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mind_control" title="Mind control">mind control</a> experimentation in order to force them give up their religious convictions. However, this was only supported by one faction within the regime, and lasted only three years. The Communist authorities closed down the prison in 1952, and punished many of those responsible for abuses (twenty of them were sentenced to death).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-communism_to_21st_century">Post-communism to 21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Post-communism to 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">collapse of the Soviet Union</a>, and the subsequent fall of communist governments across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_World" title="Orthodox World">Orthodox world</a>, there has been marked growth in Christian Orthodoxy, particularly in Russia. According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Religion_%26_Public_Life_Project" title="Pew Research Religion &amp; Public Life Project">Pew Research Religion &amp; Public Life Project</a>, between 1991 and 2008, the share of Russian adults identifying as Orthodox Christian rose from 31 percent to 72 percent, based on analysis of three waves of data (1991, 1998 and 2008) from the <a href="/wiki/International_Social_Survey_Programme" title="International Social Survey Programme">International Social Survey Programme</a> (ISSP), a collaborative effort involving social scientists in about 50 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pew research conducted in 2017 found a doubling in the global Orthodox population since the early 20th century, with the greatest resurgence in Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> In the former Soviet Union—where the largest Orthodox communities live—self-identified Orthodox Christians generally report low levels of observance and piety: In Russia, only 6% of Orthodox Christian adults reported attending church at least weekly, 15% say religion is "very important" in their lives, and 18% say they pray daily; other former Soviet republics display similarly low levels of religious observance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-129">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="1996_and_2018_Moscow.E2.80.93Constantinople_schisms"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1996_and_2018_Moscow–Constantinople_schisms">1996 and 2018 Moscow–Constantinople schisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 1996 and 2018 Moscow–Constantinople schisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1996_Moscow%E2%80%93Constantinople_schism" title="1996 Moscow–Constantinople schism">1996 Moscow–Constantinople schism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2018_Moscow%E2%80%93Constantinople_schism" title="2018 Moscow–Constantinople schism">2018 Moscow–Constantinople schism</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation_and_leadership">Organisation and leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Organisation and leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church_organization" title="Eastern Orthodox Church organization">Eastern Orthodox Church organization</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Canon_law_of_the_Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Canon law of the Eastern Orthodox Church">Canon law of the Eastern Orthodox Church</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Timeline_of_the_History_of_the_main_autocephalous_Eastern_Orthodox_Churches,_E._Orthodox_point_of_view_(2021).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1340" data-file-width="2882" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Timeline_of_the_History_of_the_main_autocephalous_Eastern_Orthodox_Churches%2C_E._Orthodox_point_of_view_%282021%29.svg/220px-Timeline_of_the_History_of_the_main_autocephalous_Eastern_Orthodox_Churches%2C_E._Orthodox_point_of_view_%282021%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Timeline_of_the_History_of_the_main_autocephalous_Eastern_Orthodox_Churches%2C_E._Orthodox_point_of_view_%282021%29.svg/330px-Timeline_of_the_History_of_the_main_autocephalous_Eastern_Orthodox_Churches%2C_E._Orthodox_point_of_view_%282021%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Timeline_of_the_History_of_the_main_autocephalous_Eastern_Orthodox_Churches%2C_E._Orthodox_point_of_view_%282021%29.svg/440px-Timeline_of_the_History_of_the_main_autocephalous_Eastern_Orthodox_Churches%2C_E._Orthodox_point_of_view_%282021%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Timeline_of_the_History_of_the_main_autocephalous_Eastern_Orthodox_Churches,_E._Orthodox_point_of_view_(2021).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Timeline showing the main autocephalous Eastern Orthodox Churches, from an Eastern Orthodox point of view, up to 2021</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Canonical_territories_of_autocephalous_and_autonomous_Eastern_Orthodox_jurisdictions_(2020).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="766" data-file-width="1469" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Canonical_territories_of_autocephalous_and_autonomous_Eastern_Orthodox_jurisdictions_%282020%29.svg/220px-Canonical_territories_of_autocephalous_and_autonomous_Eastern_Orthodox_jurisdictions_%282020%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Canonical_territories_of_autocephalous_and_autonomous_Eastern_Orthodox_jurisdictions_%282020%29.svg/330px-Canonical_territories_of_autocephalous_and_autonomous_Eastern_Orthodox_jurisdictions_%282020%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Canonical_territories_of_autocephalous_and_autonomous_Eastern_Orthodox_jurisdictions_%282020%29.svg/440px-Canonical_territories_of_autocephalous_and_autonomous_Eastern_Orthodox_jurisdictions_%282020%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Canonical_territories_of_autocephalous_and_autonomous_Eastern_Orthodox_jurisdictions_(2020).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canonical_territories" title="Canonical territories">Canonical territories</a> of the main autocephalous and autonomous Eastern Orthodox jurisdictions as of 2020</div></div></div>
<p>The Eastern Orthodox Church is a fellowship of <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a> (Greek for self-headed) churches, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecumenical_patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical patriarch of Constantinople">ecumenical patriarch of Constantinople</a> recognized as having the <i><a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">primus inter pares</a></i> status. The patriarch of Constantinople has the honor of primacy, but his title is only first among equals and has no real authority over churches other than the Constantinopolitan and set out prerogatives interpreted by the ecumenical patriarch,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-facts_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-facts-131">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> though at times the office of the ecumenical patriarch has been accused of Constantinopolitan or Eastern papism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup> The Eastern Orthodox Church considers <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus Christ</a> to be the head of the church and the church to be his body. It is believed that authority and the <a href="/wiki/Divine_grace" title="Divine grace">grace of God</a> is directly passed down to Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishops</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">clergy</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Laying_on_of_hands" title="Laying on of hands">laying on of hands</a>—a practice started by the <a href="/wiki/Apostles_in_the_New_Testament" title="Apostles in the New Testament">apostles</a>, and that this unbroken historical and physical link is an essential element of the true church (Acts 8:17, 1 Tim 4:14, Heb 6:2). The Eastern Orthodox assert that apostolic succession requires apostolic faith, and bishops without apostolic faith, who are in <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a>, forfeit their claim to apostolic succession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox communion is organised into several regional churches, which are either autocephalous ("self-headed") or lower-ranking <a href="/wiki/Autonomy_(Eastern_Orthodoxy)" title="Autonomy (Eastern Orthodoxy)">autonomous</a> (the Greek term for "self-governing") church bodies unified in <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> and worship. These include the fourteen autocephalous churches of <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Greek Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Patriarchate_of_Antioch_and_All_the_East" title="Greek Orthodox Patriarchate of Antioch and All the East">Antioch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Patriarchate_of_Jerusalem" title="Greek Orthodox Patriarchate of Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgian_Orthodox_Church" title="Georgian Orthodox Church">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Cyprus" title="Church of Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Orthodox_Church" title="Bulgarian Orthodox Church">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Orthodox_Church" title="Serbian Orthodox Church">Serbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Greece" title="Church of Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_Orthodox_Church" title="Polish Orthodox Church">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Orthodox_Church" title="Romanian Orthodox Church">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albanian_Orthodox_Church" title="Albanian Orthodox Church">Albania</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Czech_and_Slovak_Orthodox_Church" title="Czech and Slovak Orthodox Church">Czech Republic and Slovakia</a>, which were officially invited to the <a href="/wiki/Pan-Orthodox_Council" title="Pan-Orthodox Council">Pan-Orthodox Council</a> of 2016;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-council2016_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-council2016-138">[137]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church_in_America" title="Orthodox Church in America">Orthodox Church in America</a> formed in 1970; the autocephalous <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church_of_Ukraine" title="Orthodox Church of Ukraine">Orthodox Church of Ukraine</a> created in 2019; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_Orthodox_Church_%E2%80%93_Ohrid_Archbishopric" title="Macedonian Orthodox Church – Ohrid Archbishopric">Macedonian Orthodox Church – Ohrid Archbishopric</a>, granted autocephaly by Serbian Orthodox Church in 2022;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> as well as a number of autonomous churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-130">[129]</a></sup> Each church has a ruling <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishop</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Holy_Synod" title="Holy Synod">holy synod</a> to administer its jurisdiction and to lead the Eastern Orthodox Church in the preservation and teaching of the apostolic and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/patristic" title="wikt:patristic">patristic</a> traditions and church practices.
</p><p>Each bishop has a territory (<a href="/wiki/Episcopal_see" title="Episcopal see">see</a>) over which he governs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-facts_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-facts-131">[130]</a></sup> His main duty is to make sure the traditions and practices of the Eastern Orthodox Church are preserved. Bishops are equal in authority and cannot interfere in the jurisdiction of another bishop. Administratively, these bishops and their territories are organised into various <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a> groups or <a href="/wiki/Synod" title="Synod">synods</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishops</a> who gather together at least twice a year to discuss the state of affairs within their respective sees. While bishops and their autocephalous synods have the ability to administer guidance in individual cases, their actions do not usually set precedents that affect the entire Eastern Orthodox Church. Bishops are almost always chosen from the monastic ranks and must remain unmarried.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_councils">Church councils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Church councils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="1318" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg/220px-Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg/329px-Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg/439px-Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oldest extant manuscript of the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>, dated to the fifth Century</div></div></div>
<p>The ecumenical councils followed a democratic form, with each bishop having one vote. Though present and allowed to speak before the council, members of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Imperial Roman</a>/<a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> court, abbots, priests, deacons, monks and laymen were not allowed to vote. The primary goal of these great synods was to verify and confirm the fundamental beliefs of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Church" title="Great Church">Great Christian Church</a> as truth, and to remove as heresy any false teachings that would threaten the Christian Church. The <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">pope of Rome</a> at that time held the position of <i><a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">primus inter pares</a></i> ("first among equals") and, while he was not present at any of the councils, he continued to hold this title until the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a> of 1054.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other councils have helped to define the Eastern Orthodox position, specifically the <a href="/wiki/Quinisext_Council" title="Quinisext Council">Quinisext Council</a>, the Synods of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Council_of_Constantinople_(Eastern_Orthodox)" title="Fourth Council of Constantinople (Eastern Orthodox)">879–880</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Fifth Council of Constantinople">1341, 1347, 1351</a>, 1583, 1819, and <a href="/wiki/Phyletism" title="Phyletism">1872</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synod_of_Ia%C8%99i" title="Synod of Iași">Synod of Iași</a>, 1642, and the Pan-Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Jerusalem_(1672)" title="Synod of Jerusalem (1672)">Synod of Jerusalem</a>, 1672; the <a href="/wiki/Pan-Orthodox_Council" title="Pan-Orthodox Council">Pan-Orthodox Council</a>, held in Greece in 2016, was the only such Eastern Orthodox council in modern times.
</p><p>According to Eastern Orthodox teaching the position of "first among equals" gives no additional power or authority to the bishop that holds it, but rather that this person sits as organisational head of a council of equals (like a president).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the decisions made by the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">First Council of Constantinople</a> (the second ecumenical council, meeting in 381) and supported by later such councils was that the Patriarch of Constantinople should be given equal honor to the Pope of Rome since Constantinople was considered to be the "<a href="/wiki/New_Rome" title="New Rome">New Rome</a>". According to the third <a href="/wiki/Canon_(canon_law)" title="Canon (canon law)">canon</a> of the second ecumenical council: "Because [Constantinople] is new Rome, the bishop of Constantinople is to enjoy the privileges of honor after the bishop of Rome."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 28th canon of the fourth ecumenical council clarified this point by stating: "For the Fathers rightly granted privileges to the throne of Old Rome because it was the royal city. And the One Hundred and Fifty most religious Bishops (i.e. the second ecumenical council in 381) actuated by the same consideration, gave equal privileges to the most holy throne of New Rome, justly judging that the city which is honoured with the Sovereignty and the Senate, and enjoys equal privileges with the old imperial Rome, should in ecclesiastical matters also be magnified as she is."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the schism, the Eastern Orthodox no longer recognise the primacy of the pope of Rome. The patriarch of Constantinople therefore, like the Pope before him, now enjoys the title of "first among equals".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adherents">Adherents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Adherents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country" title="Eastern Orthodoxy by country">Eastern Orthodoxy by country</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Eastern_Orthodox_population.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/World_Eastern_Orthodox_population.png/500px-World_Eastern_Orthodox_population.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/World_Eastern_Orthodox_population.png/750px-World_Eastern_Orthodox_population.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/World_Eastern_Orthodox_population.png/1000px-World_Eastern_Orthodox_population.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_Eastern_Orthodox_population.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage distribution of Eastern Orthodox Christians by country</div></div></div>
<p>The most reliable estimates currently available number Eastern Orthodox adherents at around 220 million worldwide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlas_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Atlas-5">[5]</a></sup> making Eastern Orthodoxy the second largest Christian <a href="/wiki/Koinonia" title="Koinonia">communion</a> in the world after the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adherents_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-adherents-147">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[b]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the 2015 Yearbook of International Religious Demography, as of 2010, the Eastern Orthodox population was 4% of the global population, declining from 7.1% in 1910. The study also found a decrease in proportional terms, with Eastern Orthodox Christians making up 12.2% of the world's total Christian population in 2015 compared to 20.4% a century earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yearbook_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yearbook-150">[148]</a></sup> A  2017 report by the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> reached similar figures, noting that Eastern Orthodoxy has seen slower growth and less geographic spread than Catholicism and Protestantism, which were driven by <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionary</a> activity across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over two-thirds of all Eastern Orthodox members are concentrated in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Southern Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_Russia" title="Asian Russia">Russia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup> with significant minorities in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. However, Eastern Orthodoxy has become more globalized over the last century, seeing greater growth in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">the Americas</a>, and parts of Africa; churches are present in the major cities of most countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup> Adherents constitute the largest single religious <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">community</a> in Russia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sreda2012_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sreda2012-154">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[c]</a></sup>—which is home to roughly half the world's Eastern Orthodox Christians—and are the majority in Ukraine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Razumkov2006_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Razumkov2006-158">[155]</a></sup> Romania,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belarus2011_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belarus2011-159">[156]</a></sup> Greece,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[d]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-3"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-4"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-5"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-6"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-7"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/North_Macedonia" title="North Macedonia">North Macedonia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-8"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-9"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-10"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup> communities also dominate the disputed territories of <a href="/wiki/Abkhazia" title="Abkhazia">Abkhazia</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Ossetia" title="South Ossetia">South Ossetia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transnistria" title="Transnistria">Transnistria</a>. Significant Eastern Orthodox minorities exist in <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[e]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-11"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tieslietu_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tieslietu-162">[157]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[158]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2013table28_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-2013table28-164">[159]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[160]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-freedom_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-freedom-166">[161]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Albania#Religious_demography" title="Religion in Albania">Albania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-12"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup> and many other countries.
</p><p>Eastern Orthodox Christianity is the fastest growing religion in certain Western countries, primarily through labor migration from Eastern Europe, and to a lesser degree conversion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hämmerli_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hämmerli-167">[162]</a></sup> Ireland saw a doubling of its Eastern Orthodox population between 2006 and 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hämmerli_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hämmerli-167">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aughey_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aughey-168">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[164]</a></sup> Spain and Germany have the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country" title="Eastern Orthodoxy by country">largest communities in Western Europe</a>, at roughly 1.5 million each, followed by Italy with around 900,000 and France with between 500,000 and 700,000.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, four countries have over 100,000 Eastern Orthodox Christians: Canada, Mexico, Brazil, and the United States; all but the latter had fewer than 20,000 at the turn of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pewforum.org_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-pewforum.org-170">[165]</a></sup> The U.S. has seen its community more than quadruple since 1910, from 460,000 to 1.8 million as of 2017;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pewforum.org_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-pewforum.org-170">[165]</a></sup> consequently, the number of Eastern Orthodox parishes has been growing, with a 16% increase between 2000 and 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2010_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2010-171">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[f]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[g]</a></sup>
</p><p>Turkey, which for centuries once had one of the largest Eastern Orthodox communities, saw its overall Christian population fall from roughly one-fifth in 1914 to 2.5% in 1927.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[170]</a></sup> This was predominantly due to the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Dissolution of the Ottoman Empire">dissolution of the Ottoman Empire</a>, which saw most Christian territories become independent nations. The remaining Christian population was reduced further by large-scale genocides against the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_genocide" title="Armenian genocide">Armenian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">Greek</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_genocide" title="Assyrian genocide">Assyrian communities</a>; subsequent <a href="/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Greece_and_Turkey" title="Population exchange between Greece and Turkey">population exchanges between Greece and Turkey</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[171]</a></sup> and Bulgaria and Turkey; and associated <a href="/wiki/Christian_emigration" title="Christian emigration">emigration of Christians</a> to foreign countries (mostly in Europe and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Americas" title="The Americas">the Americas</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[172]</a></sup> Today, only 0.2% of Turkey's population represent either <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> or various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_denominations" title="Christian denominations">Christian denominations</a> (320,000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_156-13"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-156">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Christian_theology" title="Eastern Orthodox Christian theology">Eastern Orthodox Christian theology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trinity">Trinity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Trinity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Orthodox Christians believe in the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>, three distinct, divine persons (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypostasis_(philosophy)" title="Hypostasis (philosophy)">hypostases</a></i>), without overlap or <a href="/wiki/Sabellianism" title="Sabellianism">modality</a> among them, who each have one divine <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> (<i>ousia</i>, Greek: οὐσία)—uncreated, immaterial, and <a href="/wiki/God_and_eternity" title="God and eternity">eternal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993208–211_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993208–211-181">[174]</a></sup> These three persons are typically distinguished by their relation to each other. The <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">Father</a> is eternal and not begotten and does not proceed from any, the <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">Son</a> is eternal and begotten of the Father, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">Holy Spirit</a> is eternal and proceeds from the Father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theokritoff_2010_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theokritoff_2010-182">[175]</a></sup> Orthodox doctrine regarding the Trinity is summarised in the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed#Niceno–Constantinopolitan_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993202_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993202-183">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>Orthodox Christians believe in a <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a> <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">conception of God</a> (God is only one), which is both <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendent</a> (wholly independent of, and removed from, the material universe) and <a href="/wiki/Immanence" title="Immanence">immanent</a> (involved in the material universe).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theokritoff_2010_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-Theokritoff_2010-182">[175]</a></sup> In discussing God's relationship to his creation, Orthodox theology <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essence%E2%80%93Energies_distinction" title="Essence–Energies distinction">distinguishes</a> between God's eternal essence, which is totally transcendent, and his <i>uncreated energies</i>, which is how he reaches humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theokritoff_2010_182-2"><a href="#cite_note-Theokritoff_2010-182">[175]</a></sup> The God who is transcendent and the God who touches mankind are one and the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theokritoff_2010_182-3"><a href="#cite_note-Theokritoff_2010-182">[175]</a></sup> That is, these energies are not something that proceed from God or that God produces, but rather they are God himself: distinct, yet inseparable from God's inner being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare199367–69_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare199367–69-184">[177]</a></sup> This view is often called <a href="/wiki/Palamism" title="Palamism">Palamism</a>.
</p><p>In understanding the Trinity as "one God in three persons", "three persons" is not to be emphasised more than "one God", and vice versa. While the three persons are distinct, they are united in one divine essence, and their oneness is expressed in community and action so completely that they cannot be considered separately. For example, their salvation of mankind is an activity engaged in common: "Christ became man by the good will of the Father and by the cooperation of the Holy Spirit. Christ sends the Holy Spirit who proceeds from the Father, and the Holy Spirit forms Christ in our hearts, and thus God the Father is glorified." Their "communion of essence" is "indivisible". Trinitarian terminology—essence, hypostasis, etc.—are used "philosophically", "to answer the ideas of the heretics", and "to place the terms where they separate error and truth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHierotheos1998128–130_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHierotheos1998128–130-185">[178]</a></sup> The words do what they can do, but the nature of the Trinity in its fullness is believed to remain beyond man's comprehension and expression, a holy mystery that can only be experienced.
</p>
<h3><span id="Sin.2C_salvation.2C_and_the_incarnation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sin,_salvation,_and_the_incarnation">Sin, salvation, and the incarnation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sin, salvation, and the incarnation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:195px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Damascus_(arabic_icon).gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="574" data-file-width="364" decoding="async" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/John_Damascus_%28arabic_icon%29.gif/193px-John_Damascus_%28arabic_icon%29.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/John_Damascus_%28arabic_icon%29.gif/289px-John_Damascus_%28arabic_icon%29.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e0/John_Damascus_%28arabic_icon%29.gif 2x" width="193"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Damascus_(arabic_icon).gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_of_Damascus" title="John of Damascus">John of Damascus</a></div></div></div>
<p>When Eastern Orthodox Christians refer to fallen nature they are not saying that human nature has become evil in itself. Human nature is still formed in the image of God; humans are still God's creation, and God has never created anything evil, but fallen nature remains open to evil intents and actions. It is sometimes said among Orthodox that humans are "inclined to sin"; that is, people find some sinful things attractive. It is the nature of temptation to make sinful things seem the more attractive, and it is the fallen nature of humans that seeks or succumbs to the attraction. Orthodox Christians reject the <a href="/wiki/Augustinianism" title="Augustinianism">Augustinian position</a> that the descendants of Adam and Eve are actually guilty of the original sin of their ancestors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the fall of man, then, it has been mankind's dilemma that no human can restore his nature to union with God's grace; it was necessary for God to effect another change in human nature. Orthodox Christians believe that Christ Jesus was both God and Man absolutely and completely, having two natures indivisibly: eternally begotten of the Father in his divinity, he was born in his humanity of a woman, Mary, by her consent, through descent of the Holy Spirit. He lived on earth, in time and history, as a man. As a man he also died, and went to the place of the dead, which is <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_Hades" title="Christian views on Hades">Hades</a>. But being God, neither death nor Hades could contain him, and he rose to life again, in his humanity, by the power of the Holy Spirit, thus destroying the power of Hades and of death itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChrysostom400[[Paschal_Homily]]_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChrysostom400[[Paschal_Homily]]-187">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>Through Christ's destruction of Hades' power to hold humanity hostage, he made the path to salvation effective for all the righteous who had died from the beginning of time—saving many, including Adam and Eve, who are remembered in the church as saints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESt._Athanasius1982Ch._2–3,_p._318_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESt._Athanasius1982Ch._2–3,_p._318-188">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resurrection_of_Christ">Resurrection of Christ</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Resurrection of Christ">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="820" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg/200px-1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg/300px-1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg/399px-1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 17th-century <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox</a> icon of the Resurrection</div></div></div>
<p>The Eastern Orthodox Church understands the death and resurrection of Jesus to be real historical events, as described in the gospels of the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_life">Christian life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Christian life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Church teaching is that Orthodox Christians, through baptism, enter a new life of salvation through repentance whose purpose is to share in the life of God through the work of the Holy Spirit. The Eastern Orthodox Christian life is a spiritual pilgrimage in which each person, through the <a href="/wiki/Imitation_of_Christ" title="Imitation of Christ">imitation of Christ</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hesychasm" title="Hesychasm">hesychasm</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHierotheos1998234–237,_(241=Glossary)_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHierotheos1998234–237,_(241=Glossary)-189">[182]</a></sup> cultivates the practice of unceasing prayer. Each life occurs within the life of the church as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Body_of_Christ" title="Body of Christ">body of Christ</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeorge2006[httporthodoxinfocomgeneraltheosis-englishpdf_34]_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeorge2006[httporthodoxinfocomgeneraltheosis-englishpdf_34]-190">[183]</a></sup> It is then through the fire of God's love in the action of the Holy Spirit that each member becomes more holy, more wholly unified with Christ, starting in this life and continuing in the next.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOxford_Dict_Christian_Church3rd_ed_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOxford_Dict_Christian_Church3rd_ed-191">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[185]</a></sup> The church teaches that everyone, being born in God's image, is called to <a href="/wiki/Theosis_(Eastern_Christian_theology)" title="Theosis (Eastern Christian theology)">theosis</a>, fulfillment of the image in likeness to God. God the creator, having divinity by nature, offers each person participation in divinity by cooperatively accepting His gift of grace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeorge2006[httporthodoxinfocomgeneraltheosis-englishpdf_21]_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeorge2006[httporthodoxinfocomgeneraltheosis-englishpdf_21]-193">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox Church, in understanding itself to be the <a href="/wiki/Body_of_Christ" title="Body of Christ">Body of Christ</a>, and similarly in understanding the Christian life to lead to the unification in Christ of all members of his body, views the church as embracing all Christ's members, those now living on earth, and also all those through the ages who have passed on to the heavenly life. The church includes the Christian saints from all times, and also judges, prophets and righteous Jews of the first covenant, Adam and Eve, even the angels and heavenly hosts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHierotheos199825–30_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHierotheos199825–30-194">[187]</a></sup> In Orthodox services, the earthly members together with the heavenly members worship God as one community in Christ, in a union that transcends time and space and joins heaven to earth. This unity of the church is sometimes called the <i>communion of the saints</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHierotheos199823_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHierotheos199823-195">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virgin_Mary_and_other_saints">Virgin Mary and other saints</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Virgin Mary and other saints">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg/220px-Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg/330px-Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg/440px-Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Our_Lady_of_Tinos" title="Our Lady of Tinos">Our Lady of Tinos</a> is the major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marian_shrine" title="Marian shrine">Marian shrine</a> in Greece.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vladimirskaya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="681" data-file-width="456" decoding="async" height="329" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Vladimirskaya.jpg/220px-Vladimirskaya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Vladimirskaya.jpg/330px-Vladimirskaya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Vladimirskaya.jpg/440px-Vladimirskaya.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vladimirskaya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theotokos_of_Vladimir" title="Theotokos of Vladimir">Theotokos of Vladimir</a>, one of the most venerated of Orthodox Christian icons of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blessed_Virgin_Mary" title="Blessed Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Eastern Orthodox Church believes death and the separation of body and soul to be unnatural—a result of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fall_of_Man" title="Fall of Man">Fall of Man</a>. They also hold that the congregation of the church comprises both the living and the dead. All persons currently in heaven are considered to be <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>, whether their names are known or not. There are, however, those saints of distinction whom God has revealed as particularly good examples. When a saint is revealed and ultimately recognised by a large portion of the church a service of official recognition (<a href="/wiki/Glorification_(theology)" title="Glorification (theology)">glorification</a>) is celebrated.
</p><p>This does not "make" the person a saint; it merely recognises the fact and announces it to the rest of the church. A day is prescribed for the saint's celebration, hymns composed and icons created. Numerous saints are celebrated on each day of the year. They are venerated (shown great respect and love) but not worshipped, for worship is due God alone (this view is also held by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Oriental Orthodox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic churches</a>). In showing the saints this love and requesting their prayers, the Eastern Orthodox manifest their belief that the saints thus assist in the process of salvation for others.
</p><p>Pre-eminent among the saints is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blessed_Virgin_Mary" title="Blessed Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a> (commonly referred to as <i>Theotokos</i> or <i>Bogoroditsa</i>: "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mother_of_God" title="Mother of God">Mother of God</a>"). In <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_theology" title="Eastern Orthodox theology">Eastern Orthodox theology</a>, the Mother of God is the fulfillment of the Old Testament archetypes revealed in the <a href="/wiki/Ark_of_the_Covenant" title="Ark of the Covenant">Ark of the Covenant</a> (because she carried the New Covenant in the person of Christ) and the <a href="/wiki/Burning_bush" title="Burning bush">burning bush</a> that appeared before <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> (symbolizing the Mother of God's carrying of God without being consumed).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox believe that Christ, from the moment of his conception, was both fully God and fully human. Mary is thus called the <i>Theotokos</i> or <i>Bogoroditsa</i> as an affirmation of the divinity of the one to whom she gave birth. It is also believed that her virginity was not compromised in conceiving God-incarnate, that she was not harmed and that she remained forever a virgin. Scriptural references to "brothers" of Christ are interpreted as kin, given that the word "brother" was used in multiple ways, as was the term "father". Due to her unique place in salvation history, Mary is honoured above all other saints and especially venerated for the great work that God accomplished through her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993257–258_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993257–258-197">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox Church regards the bodies of all saints as holy, made such by participation in the holy mysteries, especially the communion of Christ's holy body and blood, and by the indwelling of the Holy Spirit within the church. Indeed, that persons and physical things can be made holy is a cornerstone of the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">Incarnation</a>, made manifest also directly by God in Old Testament times through his dwelling in the Ark of the Covenant. Thus, physical items connected with saints are also regarded as holy, through their participation in the earthly works of those saints. According to church teaching and tradition, God himself bears witness to this holiness of saints' <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a> through the many miracles connected with them that have been reported throughout history since biblical times, often including healing from disease and injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993234_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993234-198">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eschatology">Eschatology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Eschatology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">Christian eschatology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3684" data-file-width="2760" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg/200px-Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg/300px-Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg/400px-Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Last Judgment</i>: 12th-century <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> mosaic from <a href="/wiki/Torcello" title="Torcello">Torcello</a> Cathedral</div></div></div>
<p>Orthodox Christians believe that when a person dies the soul is temporarily separated from the body. Though it may linger for a short period on Earth, it is ultimately escorted either to paradise (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abraham%27s_bosom" title="Abraham's bosom">Abraham's bosom</a>) or the darkness of <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_Hades" title="Christian views on Hades">Hades</a>, following the <a href="/wiki/Particular_judgment" title="Particular judgment">Temporary Judgment</a>. Orthodox do not accept the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">Purgatory</a>, which is held by Catholicism. The soul's experience of either of these states is only a "foretaste"—being experienced only by the soul—until the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Final Judgment</a>, when the soul and body will be reunited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soul_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-soul-200">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox believe that the state of the soul in Hades can be affected by the love and prayers of the righteous up until the Last Judgment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[194]</a></sup> For this reason the Church offers a special <a href="/wiki/Prayer_for_the_dead" title="Prayer for the dead">prayer for the dead</a> on the third day, ninth day, fortieth day, and the one-year anniversary after the death of an Orthodox Christian. There are also several days throughout the year that are set aside for general commemoration of the departed, sometimes including nonbelievers. These days usually fall on a Saturday, since it was on a Saturday that Christ lay in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">Tomb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soul_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-soul-200">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox believe that after the Final Judgment:
</p>
<ul><li>All souls will be reunited with their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_Dead" title="Resurrection of the Dead">resurrected bodies</a>.</li>
<li>All souls will fully experience their spiritual state.</li>
<li>Having been perfected, the saints will forever progress towards a deeper and fuller love of God, which equates with eternal happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soul_200-2"><a href="#cite_note-soul-200">[193]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bible">Bible</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Bible">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
